  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_e3890510-97a9-44b8-bbde-9484d87cbb72'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5674026144417422244">A captivating read from a debut novelist, <i>Brick Lane</i> brings the immigrant milieu of East London to vibrant life. With great poignancy, Ali illuminates a foreign world; her well-developed characters pull readers along on a deeply psychological, almost spiritual journey. Through the eyes of two Bangladeshi sisters—the plain Nazneen and the prettier Hasina—we see the divergen</span>
  <span id="freeText5674026144417422244" style="display:none">A captivating read from a debut novelist, <i>Brick Lane</i> brings the immigrant milieu of East London to vibrant life. With great poignancy, Ali illuminates a foreign world; her well-developed characters pull readers along on a deeply psychological, almost spiritual journey. Through the eyes of two Bangladeshi sisters—the plain Nazneen and the prettier Hasina—we see the divergent paths of the contemporary descendants of an ancient culture. Hasina elopes to a "love marriage," and young Nazneen, in an arranged marriage, is pledged to a much older man living in London. <br /><br />Ali's skillful narrative focuses on Nazneen's stifling life with her ineffectual husband, who keeps her imprisoned in a city housing project filled with immigrants in varying degrees of assimilation. But Ali reveals a bittersweet tension between the "two kinds of love" Nazneen and her sister experience—that which begins full and overflowing, only to slowly dissipate, and another which emerges like a surprise, growing unexpectedly over years of faithful commitment. Both of these loves have their own pitfalls: Hasina's passionate romance crumbles into domestic violence, and Nazneen's marriage never quite reaches a state of wedded bliss.<br /><br />Though comparisons have drawn between Ali and Zadie Smith, a better comparison might be made between this talented newcomer and the work of Amy Tan, who so deftly portrays the immigrant experience with empathy and joy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5674026144417422244" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2940416" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2940416" />
    <a title="Nancy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65675-nancy"><img alt="Nancy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2940416?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy" href="/user/show/65675-nancy">Nancy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">somebody who wants to read it</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2940416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13886640029554237858">Could it take me longer to read a book? I made myself read this book everyday so I could be done with it and properly hate it.<br/><br/>Look at what the <i>NY Review of Books</i> said:<br/><br/>"Ali succeeds brilliantly in presenting the besieged humanity of people living hard, little-known lives on the margins of a rich, self-absorbed society."<br/><br/>WHO IS THIS CRAZY NUT? You need to read a book like Brick Lane to understand "besieged humanity" or what it's like to live a "hard, little-known" life? <br/><br/>The protaganist moves around</span>
  <span id="freeText13886640029554237858" style="display:none">Could it take me longer to read a book? I made myself read this book everyday so I could be done with it and properly hate it.<br /><br />Look at what the <i>NY Review of Books</i> said:<br /><br />"Ali succeeds brilliantly in presenting the besieged humanity of people living hard, little-known lives on the margins of a rich, self-absorbed society."<br /><br />WHO IS THIS CRAZY NUT? You need to read a book like Brick Lane to understand "besieged humanity" or what it's like to live a "hard, little-known" life? <br /><br />The protaganist moves around in the book like she's had a lobotomy. It wasn't until page 152, I believe, when Nazneen giggles. FINALLY, the woman shows a sign of life. Her senses are completely dulled. Don't buy into the crap about "what it must be like to live a suppressed/oppressed life as a Muslim woman." That's not what's going on. Compare Nazneen's character to that of her sister, aunt, and friends. It's a wonder that lifeless Nazneen even moves into an affair with a younger man. <br /><br />The most ridiculous part (and because of that, maybe the most enjoyable?) of Nazneen's story is when she stops this evil money lender Mafia-like woman in the story by asking her to swear on the Qu'ran. Really, is that all it took? This woman is an interest-charging money lender (which, apparently, is a big NO-NO as a Muslim) who runs a religious school for girls and raises her sons to be repo-man thugs but she's afraid to swear on the Qu'ran because of an accounting discrepancy? Score one for Nazneen and her growing independence!<br /><br />OK, <b>to be fair</b>, Nazneen is supposed to be a woman who is passive. Her own mother left Nazneen's entrance into the world up to Fate and that fatalism is what she was raised on. But how she moved from that passivity into an affair with a younger man? Kind of muddy. And her decision to stand up to her husband and stay in the UK with the kids? Little murky. But why should I nitpick? Who knows why we do anything in this world?<br /><br />Back to being unfair (because I was so bored by this book)...<br /><br />If you read it, you'll actually end up sympathizing with her husband Chanu..seriously. If you've started reading the book, you will understand what I mean. I know he's supposed to be some idiot windbag who talks like a bigshot at home but deals with the disappointed fragments of his dreams outside the home, but do we need 200 pages of his pathetic flaps to understand this??? And, if one could isolate the number of sentences or paragraphs that concerned the corns on his feet, could there be about 20 pages? Is this the same "brilliant book about things that matter" that Ian Jack of <i>Granta</i> refers to? People! Puh-leeze give me a break!<br /><br /><b>THE ONE REALLY GOOD THING ABOUT THIS BOOK</b> is the story about Nazneen's sister. She writes letters to her sister detailing her life in Dhaka. The character Hasina is everything that Nazneen is not: angry, sad, happy, determined, loving, and alive. Her life is amazing. The letters alone saved the book. However, even this was ruined by Monica Ali. Why did Hasina's letters need to be written in some strange broken English or literally translated Bengali? If we can view Nazneen's life through grammatically correct English, why can't we understand Hasina in something gramatically correct? Is this to emphasize her distance? Whatever. Weird and frustrating to read. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="13886640029554237858" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I kept back from reading this until 1984 and oh my, it blows my mind even today, especially today with all that is going on in the world. Books like 1984, Brave New World, We and The Handmaid's Tale have iconic status for good reason...because they are the very best in their genre. <span id="freeTextContainer17080419308221101114">I don't know why they do it but they do it a lot - on the title page it says<br/><br/><i>Brick Lane : A Novel</i> <br/><br/>And there I was expecting this oblong of printed material to be<br/><br/><i>Brick Lane : A New Kind of Vacuum Cleaner</i> <br/><br/>Anyway. Other reviews would have you believe that this book is terrifically boring, beaten only for tediousness by <b>Some Variations in the Major Groups of Plankton of the Kamchatka Peninsula Littoral</b> by R.K. Litkynshovskaya and P.I. Podgorna-Bialaczczka. So why did I really enjoy this novel? Could </span>
  <span id="freeText17080419308221101114" style="display:none">I don't know why they do it but they do it a lot - on the title page it says<br /><br /><i>Brick Lane : A Novel</i> <br /><br />And there I was expecting this oblong of printed material to be<br /><br /><i>Brick Lane : A New Kind of Vacuum Cleaner</i> <br /><br />Anyway. Other reviews would have you believe that this book is terrifically boring, beaten only for tediousness by <b>Some Variations in the Major Groups of Plankton of the Kamchatka Peninsula Littoral</b> by R.K. Litkynshovskaya and P.I. Podgorna-Bialaczczka. So why did I really enjoy this novel? Could it be that after a while I accepted my fate in the same way our heroine accepts hers, and my heart, like hers, fluttered when the slightest thing out of the ordinary happened? Or maybe I'm a Samuel Beckett fan and don't realise it. It's very true I do love the music of Steve Reich, which could never be described as dramatic, and indeed has often been compared to <b>Some Variations in the Major Groups of Plankton of the Kamchatka Peninsula Littoral.</b> But really I think I prefer the company of Nazneen and her very aggravating husband Chanu over, say, Jean Claude Van Damme and Steven Seagal any day of the week. Not to mention most of my work colleagues and family members. Of course it may be true that should Monica Ali choose to write a graceful and compassionate novel about any of that rabble, I'd be glued to that too. </span>

<div id="review_3353884557" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3353884557" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3353884557?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552771153" href="/book/show/117664.Brick_Lane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=21st-century">21st-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=british-isles">british-isles</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3353884557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10861031883104086820">That was not what I expected.<br/><br/>Not that I can define very well what I did expect. I am curious that Ali, after making a splash with this book and writing a few more novels has pretty much disappeared.<br/><br/>I supposed I imagined that this book would very strongly a novel of the New Labour era that by now would be well past it's best before date and that it would smell dated and stale. It is dated in that I could see a novelist tackling the same topic might be angrier and there would not be minor touches </span>
  <span id="freeText10861031883104086820" style="display:none">That was not what I expected.<br /><br />Not that I can define very well what I did expect. I am curious that Ali, after making a splash with this book and writing a few more novels has pretty much disappeared.<br /><br />I supposed I imagined that this book would very strongly a novel of the New Labour era that by now would be well past it's best before date and that it would smell dated and stale. It is dated in that I could see a novelist tackling the same topic might be angrier and there would not be minor touches of optimism like the estate getting a youth centre and the main character's flat finally getting some repairs, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[possibly this 'modern' version I imagine might tie up the Bangladesh and Tower Hamlets stories courtesy of the Bangladesh Awami League  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />What is this is, is a very close portrait of a woman over time, another review mentioned <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2175.Madame_Bovary" title="Madame Bovary by Gustave Flaubert" rel="nofollow noopener">Madame Bovary</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15823480.Anna_Karenina" title="Anna Karenina by Leo Tolstoy" rel="nofollow noopener">Anna Karenina</a>, well yes there is a connection, but that would be a spoiler and in terms of the drive of the story it is only a part of the main character's journey not a decisive (and fatal) hammer blow. Ice Skating and Torvil &amp; Dean are more important ultimately in this story.<br /> <br />My sense was that this is much more like a version of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/188454.The_Old_Wives__Tale" title="The Old Wives' Tale by Arnold Bennett" rel="nofollow noopener">The old wives tale</a>, the contrasting lives of two sisters, the adventurous and beautiful Hasina, and her elder sister the dutiful and eager to please Nazneen. The two are brought up in a village in Bangladesh, although <i>at first</i> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and that is significant, because this is an evolutionary novel, all about change over time <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Nazneen, who is married of to an older man and goes with him to London, hankers after that lost world of childhood, Ali is careful not to show us the village as a bucolic idyll. Still it is what Nazneen knew.We learn of Hasina's story through the occasional letters that she sends Nazneen and this is used to advance the story by a few years at one stage.<br /><br />This novel is a great portrayal of London too - not as a teeming, pigeon infested Metropolis with it's sewers full of looming Fatbergs, but as the tight world of a one bedroom flat (later a two bedroom flat) in a shabby council housing estate in Tower Hamlets, when we see; Bishopsgate, Covent Garden or Buckingham Palace they are strange, alien places, other worlds in every sense.<br /><br />I felt also that aside from some jokes, that this was a Victorian novel, the main character is suspiciously reliable, it is mostly scrupulously chronological - flashbacks are clear, there is no whiff of modernism, in literary terms this is a continuation of the novel as Dickens knew it, it is something like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/58696.David_Copperfield" title="David Copperfield by Charles Dickens" rel="nofollow noopener">David Copperfield</a>, very domestic, starting from the birth running from misery towards non-misery with up's and downs on the way, the wicked Loan Shark - an increasingly elderly Bengali woman with two dim sons as her enforcers, is a Dickensian grotesque as is Nazneen's hapless husband, overweight, with his corns and unheard thesis on white working class racism - though Ali treats him far more tenderly than Dickens' would have, he is some of a Mr Micawber believing that 'something will come up', a promotion, a job, business opportunities - his narrative arc is <i>very</i> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ sorry for the spoiler <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> similar too. He does get to be the voice of truth and knowledge, despite being a ridiculous and often silly figure in the novel.<br /><br />And then there are jokes <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ ok, not particularly funny ones <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, Nazneen's husband works for Mr Dalloway but is never invited round to his house for the barbecue hosted by his wife, there is a Dr Azad, like his namesake in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45195.A_Passage_to_India" title="A Passage to India by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">A Passage to India</a> ashamed of his own house <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ this novel could have been called <i> A Passage to England</i> - it is very largely Forster's book turned inside out <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ but I am just showering you with spoilers here <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Clothes are a nice feature of this novel both as character or character's statements of allegiance and outlook. Certainly a good, solid novel, it may not turn your life upside down or keep you awake at night, but is very nicely done.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10861031883104086820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/806742433" />
    <a title="Alesa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/889171-alesa"><img alt="Alesa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469337165p2/889171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/806742433?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alesa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alesa" href="/user/show/889171-alesa">Alesa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer806742433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2456882131235804754">There's a good reason that Brick Lane was short-listed for the Man Booker award, and was nominated for a whole slew of other prizes too. It is just brilliant. That doesn't mean that it is necessarily fun to read. (A 16 year old Bangladeshi girl is married off to a 40 year old guy in London, and goes there to start a new life in almost poverty. No, not exactly a "fun" topic…) However, the descriptions are brilliant, and the story itself is mesmerizing. The subplots are rich and believable. You re</span>
  <span id="freeText2456882131235804754" style="display:none">There's a good reason that Brick Lane was short-listed for the Man Booker award, and was nominated for a whole slew of other prizes too. It is just brilliant. That doesn't mean that it is necessarily fun to read. (A 16 year old Bangladeshi girl is married off to a 40 year old guy in London, and goes there to start a new life in almost poverty. No, not exactly a "fun" topic…) However, the descriptions are brilliant, and the story itself is mesmerizing. The subplots are rich and believable. You really feel like you've learned a lot about what it means to be a Blangladeshi immigrant to the UK.<br /><br />However, it does a lot more, namely it urges the reader to think about:<br /><br />* The imposition of passivity on a woman, to the point of making passivity a virtue, something that certainly transcends cultures and limits self-expectations (a theme throughout the book is the main character being left to her Fate at birth)<br />* The experience of any immigrant, and the complex attitudes that the immigrant experience generates both towards the new culture and the old<br />* The deplorable status of women in Bangladesh<br />* The wrenching realization that one is married to an idiot<br />* The nature of marriage itself<br />* The hypocrites that exist in any culture and religion (very ironic that the shameless usurer in the book is named Mrs. Islam)<br />* The way that living in a specific slice of history influences one's opinions and values<br /><br />Although this book is rather long, the author probably needed the length to address her varied themes. It's a serious book, but often hilarious. It's uplifting, because it speaks of women's empowerment. And it kindled a desire in me to learn more about Bangladesh and its history -- and perhaps go there some day.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2456882131235804754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1428668434" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1428668434" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7477266-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433820623p2/7477266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1428668434?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/7477266-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385604840" href="/book/show/884655.Brick_Lane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7477266-sarah?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7477266-sarah?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7477266-sarah?shelf=z2015">z2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1428668434" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer366999145096864775">I thought this book was really interesting as it gave an insight into being an immigrant in England and it also gave insights into life in Bangladesh. Of course, Monica Ali has been scrutinised because she doesn't speak fluent Bangladeshi etc and I know nothing about the being an immigrant myself but I felt like the representation she gave felt really authentic. <br/><br/>I thought the characters were brilliant. They were really interesting and I felt like nearly every one of them added to the story. They</span>
  <span id="freeText366999145096864775" style="display:none">I thought this book was really interesting as it gave an insight into being an immigrant in England and it also gave insights into life in Bangladesh. Of course, Monica Ali has been scrutinised because she doesn't speak fluent Bangladeshi etc and I know nothing about the being an immigrant myself but I felt like the representation she gave felt really authentic. <br /><br />I thought the characters were brilliant. They were really interesting and I felt like nearly every one of them added to the story. They weren't just devices to the main character, in fact, I would say Nazneen was sort of like a device for them to be portrayed. Nazneen was a silent observer. The amount of things she observed was unreal. Even if a character didn't say much, Nazneen observed what they were doing physically and that gave us such an insight into characters. I feel like Nazneen was the way she was because Ali wanted her that way. I thought Nazneen's inner-monologue was similar to that of a third person narration that sees all. <br /><br />The story was kind of slow but for good reason. Nothing thrilling or exciting happened and that's because it mainly focused on a family. So because of that the pacing was sometimes unbearable but I can understand why. I actually liked the ending a lot. I think it ends with a bit of hope and maybe that's a bit of a cop-out but I'm a sucker for hopeful endings. <br /><br />I would recommend this book to people who think that they'll enjoy it but just be aware it's quite slow. I would probably read another book by Monica Ali. </span>
  <a data-text-id="366999145096864775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_364320720" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/364320720" />
    <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1"><img alt="Shovelmonkey1" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391713268p2/3926982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/364320720?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shovelmonkey1" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1">Shovelmonkey1</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385606738" href="/book/show/884671.Brick_Lane">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people with  microscopes</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Shovelmonkey1 by: </span>
      i like brightly coloured book covers
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=almost-chick-lit">almost-chick-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=islam">islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer364320720" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18337194368574229491">This is not what I was expecting. Don’t ask me what I was expecting because it is not a definable quantity and defies explanation but when I bought this book on a whim because I liked the juxtaposition of white background and colourful printed letters, this was not it.<br/><br/>Ali has created a book for those who love the microscopic and want a very detailed picture of a very limited section of space and time. Hold on you might say, this book moves from 1985 and Nazneen’s arrival in England all the way u</span>
  <span id="freeText18337194368574229491" style="display:none">This is not what I was expecting. Don’t ask me what I was expecting because it is not a definable quantity and defies explanation but when I bought this book on a whim because I liked the juxtaposition of white background and colourful printed letters, this was not it.<br /><br />Ali has created a book for those who love the microscopic and want a very detailed picture of a very limited section of space and time. Hold on you might say, this book moves from 1985 and Nazneen’s arrival in England all the way up to and beyond 9/11 so how can that be microscopic. That’s a good 15 years plus some. Yes, it does cover this ground but it covers this ground in the same way that Bill and Ted covered all the major periods of history in their righteous phone box. By jumping about a lot with no real overall picture. But anyway it’s obvious that Monica Ali did not set out to create a worldly overview. Instead it is and was always about Nazneen. And it is through the eyes of Nazneen and her sisters epistles that the story is told. <br /><br />What’s the story? Primarily that life as a bride in an arranged marriage, on a council estate in a country where you don’t speak the language and hold no currency (I think I may have accidentally started quoting a Paul Simon song but never mind), is going to be largely devoid of joy. And it is up to a point. However the other side of the coin is that joy can be found in the most unpromising of places and under surprising circumstances and that just because your life is one way, does not mean it always has to be so. The power of change lies within you. Shocked? Really, you’re not? I wasn’t either but hey, it’s part of the story so it has to go in the review.<br /><br />The cast of characters who are fully fleshed out are fairly limited; Nazneen, her husband Chanu and their two daughters Shahana and Bibi are all well developed -you might find them dull, but that’s another issue. Karim is an object of lust with eminently describable forearms and mercurial dress sense and the usurer Mrs Islam, friendly Razia, and unemotional Dr Azad are all quite memorable.<br /><br />For me, youngest daughter Bibi was the stand-out character (she is the reason for the third star). She doesn’t say anything - well maybe a couple of lines but Monica Ali imbued her with a sense of personality purely through the descriptions of her physical stance, her hair-chewing proclivities and her general watchfulness that gave me a greater sense of her motivations and personality than all of Chanu’s chatter or Mrs Islam’s outrageous theatrics.<br /> <br />If you want a big story where a lot happens then you need to walk away from this book immediately or be endlessly annoyed. If you want a tiny but very detailed slice of life pie to chew on then bring a plate and a fork because you’ve found it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18337194368574229491" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4254400?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nitya Iyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nitya Iyer" href="/user/show/78611-nitya-iyer">Nitya Iyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780743243308" href="/book/show/536972.Brick_Lane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78611-nitya-iyer?shelf=1star">1star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4254400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12313905725020498571">I desperately wanted to like this book. Having lived the immigrant, foreigner, displaced person lifestyle for so long, I wanted this book to capture everything that it means to have lost links with my own personal history in the effort to fit into the culture that's welcomed me into it's monied bosom. <br/><br/>But Nazneen is not me. She's a village girl without education and more importantly, the confidence education brings to a traveller navigating a foreign world.<br/><br/>I snacked with her in the dead of night</span>
  <span id="freeText12313905725020498571" style="display:none">I desperately wanted to like this book. Having lived the immigrant, foreigner, displaced person lifestyle for so long, I wanted this book to capture everything that it means to have lost links with my own personal history in the effort to fit into the culture that's welcomed me into it's monied bosom. <br /><br />But Nazneen is not me. She's a village girl without education and more importantly, the confidence education brings to a traveller navigating a foreign world.<br /><br />I snacked with her in the dead of night, desperate to fill a void opened when I left my home and all things familiar. But when she wandered into meeting of revolutionaries looking for acceptance, I stood at the door and wondered whether she had left her brain at home. <br /><br />And really, why are her sister's letters from home so poorly written. I've never seen a native speaker butcher her own language to the point of becoming incomprehensible as brutally as Hasina does. Of course I worry about the poor girl struggling to face the fate she foolishly chose for herself, but must she do it in such a jarring manner. <br /><br />I wish I hadn't bother to finish the book. I knew how it was going to end early on, but like looking to see what happened in a car crash, I couldn't tear my eyes until the book was over.<br /><br />My time was wasted. And that I resent most of all</span>

          </span>

    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<div id="review_16857322" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16857322" />
    <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days"><img alt="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420332941p2/395599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16857322?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days">Shannon (Giraffe Days)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=2008">2008</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16857322" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7125601822768173990">Nazneen is the eldest of two girls, growing up in a village in Bangladesh. Her younger sister Hasina runs away to marry the young man she is in love with, and not long after that, when she is eighteen, Nazneen is married to a man twenty years older than her and sent to live with him in London.<br/><br/>Her husband, Chanu, is kind and very talkative. They live in a dingy flat on an estate where she makes friends with some other Bangladeshi women. Her world is narrow and small, consisting of the flat and Br</span>
  <span id="freeText7125601822768173990" style="display:none">Nazneen is the eldest of two girls, growing up in a village in Bangladesh. Her younger sister Hasina runs away to marry the young man she is in love with, and not long after that, when she is eighteen, Nazneen is married to a man twenty years older than her and sent to live with him in London.<br /><br />Her husband, Chanu, is kind and very talkative. They live in a dingy flat on an estate where she makes friends with some other Bangladeshi women. Her world is narrow and small, consisting of the flat and Brick Lane, where she walks one step behind her husband. She is not encouraged to learn English, or even leave the estate. But gradually she moves more and more into the world outside, though all that she knows is what Chanu tells her - and he "is an educated man", as he is constantly saying. They have three children, two girls after the first little boy dies, and some twelve years after she arrived in England she is swept up in an affair with a young man, Karim. Slowly, so slowly, she begins to speak for herself, but always there is this need to be a good, dutiful wife. To cut the skin around the corns on her husband's feet, to cook and keep the flat tidy, to be Chanu's audience as he lectures, to watch as his plans and ambitions fall flat one by one.<br /><br />The story is told from Nazneen's perspective, sliced through with letters from her sister in Dhaka, whose story is easily more tragic. Nazneen, quiet and unknowledgable, is like a blank canvass for the opinions and impressions of others. When she asks a direct question, rarely is it answered. We see her world, small as it is though at times shaken by greater deeds (like nine-eleven), through her watchful, patient eyes. Many things are shown rather than told, making the real situation easily discernible and very rich and layered.<br /><br />It is beautifully, skilfully written. A bit slow maybe, but with great impact. I can't deny that it didn't affect me, and bring me down a bit at times. The story slips into a secret place, shines a light on a place generally ignored and dismissed and undervalued: the housewife's domain and life. The politics, the aspirations, the hatred against Muslims, the clash of cultures and struggle to assimilate without abandoning your own culture, it's all there. But through it all there is strength in these women, and determination. The struggle Nazneen goes through, with her own conscience, her own desires and wants so long put aside, her fondness for the often revolting - but not cruel, no, he never beats her - Chanu (a surprisingly sympathetic character, in that it's easy to feel sorry for him), while gang wars and drug abuse and abusive husbands play out in both England and Bangladesh, all creates a vivid portrayal of an immigrant experience common to London, Paris, Sydney, Toronto. Relevant, topical, at times heartarchingly sad, Brick Lane is like Nazneen, watching silently, presenting a story without spoken judgement, biding its time, and at the end, so very very rewarding.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7125601822768173990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1983200788" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1983200788" />
    <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads"><img alt="Abbie | ab_reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545340790p2/53055819.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1983200788?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abbie | ab_reads" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads">Abbie | ab_reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552771153" href="/book/show/117664.Brick_Lane">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1983200788" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5989737203077085449">This book left me with quite mixed feelings to be honest. I wanted to love it, it’s been compared to White Teeth by Zadie Smith which is one of my all time faves, but I thought it lacked the vibrancy and liveliness of White Teeth, despite both of them portraying the lives of immigrants in London.<br/>.<br/>Brick Lane follows Nazneen, a Bangladeshi woman who moves to London for an arranged marriage. I think the slowness of the book comes from the passivity of Nazneen, as she doesn’t really do much for much</span>
  <span id="freeText5989737203077085449" style="display:none">This book left me with quite mixed feelings to be honest. I wanted to love it, it’s been compared to White Teeth by Zadie Smith which is one of my all time faves, but I thought it lacked the vibrancy and liveliness of White Teeth, despite both of them portraying the lives of immigrants in London.<br />.<br />Brick Lane follows Nazneen, a Bangladeshi woman who moves to London for an arranged marriage. I think the slowness of the book comes from the passivity of Nazneen, as she doesn’t really do much for much of the first half. Her husband, Chanu, is such an interesting character though. He is tragic, annoying, inspiring, and hilarious all at once, and I really felt for him trying to find an outlet for his passions in a system where he doesn’t feel appreciated!<br />.<br />My favourite parts of the book were the passages describing anecdotes from Nazneen’s childhood in Bangladesh - I wish there were more of them! The letters from Hasina, Nazneen’s sister, were also interesting (not to mention heartbreaking) but the extremely stilted English that they’re written in is off-putting and tiresome to read. It seemed to me that the first batch were written far better than all the others - her writing seemed to deteriorate, I thought it was inconsistent.<br />.<br />Overall, plenty of compelling parts, such as Nazneen’s friend Razia’s struggle with her wayward son, but too many dry patches that dragged, and a romantic subplot that I didn’t care for. Ali did a great job of depicting the rising racial tensions in the early 2000s though!<br />.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5989737203077085449" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_743526518" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/743526518" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/743526518?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=multiculture">multiculture</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer743526518" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3753423051497966432">I did enjoy this novel; it goes at a good pace and there is a warmth about it that I appreciated. The structure of the novel is interesting. Nazneen is born in a village in Bangladesh; when old enough she is married to Chanu, a much older man who lives in England. She goes to England as a bride in her teens in 1985. The story follows her over the next years (until 2002) as she has children and mixes with the Bangladeshi community around Brick Lane. The novel also cuts to her sister Hasina back i</span>
  <span id="freeText3753423051497966432" style="display:none">I did enjoy this novel; it goes at a good pace and there is a warmth about it that I appreciated. The structure of the novel is interesting. Nazneen is born in a village in Bangladesh; when old enough she is married to Chanu, a much older man who lives in England. She goes to England as a bride in her teens in 1985. The story follows her over the next years (until 2002) as she has children and mixes with the Bangladeshi community around Brick Lane. The novel also cuts to her sister Hasina back in Bangladesh periodically. There are memorable characters in the Bangladeshi community, each coping with being in a strange culture in different ways; some by blending in others by keeping apart.<br />Nazneen’s husband Chanu turns out to be a decent man (he doesn’t beat her); he wants a simple village girl to look after him and doesn’t allow Nazneen to learn English, as she doesn’t need it. The novel is tragic and comic, although the comedy is restrained, it is still there. Ali describes physicality very well; you do get a sense of the characters by the descriptions of physical habits and tics, by the way they wear their clothes, fiddle with their hair and so on.<br />Nazneen develops as the novel goes on and gradually one gets a sense of her becoming rounded as an individual, liberated almost. There are also grand themes; religion and its relation to culture, characters cut off from their origins and adrift in a foreign land, adultery, poverty, family tensions; all the stuff of everyday life and high drama. There are correlations with Madame Bovary and Anna Karenina. It is about how much control we have over our lives; can you go back to a dream? It isn’t a simple clash of cultures; it is more nuanced; even Karim, a devout Muslim, is a complex and interesting character. Brick Lane is also a novel about place and the geographical restriction of Nazneen’s life adds to the power of her character development. I know this isn’t a universally loved novel, but I enjoyed it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3753423051497966432" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11643650077223380470" style="display:none">It's a bit draconian to give a book that sells so well only one star, but that's my rating for a book I don't make it through. I read a full third of this book waiting for the protagonist (Nanzeen) to be interesting and it didn't happen. The one highlight was the small window into Bengali/Pakistani culture (before chapter 2 moves to Britain). It's a book about fate and how one acts as a follower in life. And the exceedingly slow learning process Nanzeen goes through when she starts to discover she can shape her life and maybe that's not wrong. Actually we didn't get quite that far in a third of the book. Needless to say I didn't identify much with the main character, and reading about her abhorrent life was just too much work for what is meant to be an enjoyable activity. I'll blame it on my book group.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2361872408" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2361872408" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11850609595363727529">Monica Ali is able to capture  the sense of discombobulation felt by both both first generation immigrants; whether it is the Shakespeare-loving Chanu, who on the one hand sees himself as a lover of English literature, a sensitive, educated and artistically minded man who missed his calling as the Private Secretary to the Prime Minister, but on the hand deeply feels the deep-seated prejudices he experiences in Britain and begins to hate the modernity taking over the country, a kind of walking co</span>
  <span id="freeText11850609595363727529" style="display:none">Monica Ali is able to capture  the sense of discombobulation felt by both both first generation immigrants; whether it is the Shakespeare-loving Chanu, who on the one hand sees himself as a lover of English literature, a sensitive, educated and artistically minded man who missed his calling as the Private Secretary to the Prime Minister, but on the hand deeply feels the deep-seated prejudices he experiences in Britain and begins to hate the modernity taking over the country, a kind of walking contradiction he harks after an idyllic British past which never existed, yet resents the Westernisation of a Bengali culture which he, outside a love of Tagore, he is never really a part on.  On the other hand is his wife Nazneen, or whom marriage at first is a kind of trap-for all his profession for being a liberal, Chanu represents some of the worst aspects of misogyny of  first generation Bangladeshi immigrants; rarely allowed to leave the house, not given permission to be educated, Nazneen feels trapped in a country she not only cannot, but is not allowed to comprehend. On top of this she feels emotionally stifled by becoming a sounding board for Chanu's frequently sententious rambles which she is unable to fully comprehend. Gradually she is able to gain a sense of self, culminating in an affair with the energetic if somewhat mediocre Karim; this acts as a catalyst for her self-actualisation, that she is able to, for the first time in her life, treated as a fully rounded human being and able to experience emotions outside the sense of obedience expected of her, that she is no longer just a daughter, wife or sister but a woman with her own sense of independence. Naturally, like her early attempts at marital rebellion when Chanu rejects her request for her sister to move to England, her affair goes un-noticed by her husband who meanders through life labouring under a series of illusions, blind to what is going on around him. It is difficult to describe the relationship which exists between the two, Chanu, despite the bluster and blindness seems to genuinely care for Nazneen, likewise Nazneen, despite the occasional sense of disgust she feels for him, seems to value the innate sense of kindness in Chanu, the sense of comfort he brings. In many ways Ali describes the emotions which marriages, especially arranged ones, often bring; comfort and convenience.<br /><br /> The secondary characters who inhabit the novel, from Chanu and Nazneen's daughters Shahana and Bibi to Mrs Islam help add depth to the novel-most of the secondary characters represent an important part of the immigrant experience, whether it be clashes with narrow-minded nationalists or acclimatising to life as a second-generation Bangladeshi girl in London, 'Brick Lane' is a nuanced and well-written depiction of two generations of a Bangladeshi family in a fast-changing world. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11850609595363727529" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3359707723" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3359707723" />
    <a title="robin friedman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman"><img alt="robin friedman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571353109p2/22269025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3359707723?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="robin friedman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="robin friedman" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman">robin friedman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552771153" href="/book/show/117664.Brick_Lane">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3359707723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14427790336609882780">Monica Ali's "Brick Lane"<br/><br/>This lengthy and ambitious first novel explores, with indifferent success, the lives of Bangladeshi immigrants to London and the growth in independence and modification of culture of a young Bangladeshi woman.<br/><br/>The heroine of the book is Nazneen who at the age of 20 enters an arranged marriage with Chanu, age 40, a Banladeshi struggling to establish himself in London. Chanu is striving for a promotion, is proud of his attempts to secure education, and is portrayed at the o</span>
  <span id="freeText14427790336609882780" style="display:none">Monica Ali's "Brick Lane"<br /><br />This lengthy and ambitious first novel explores, with indifferent success, the lives of Bangladeshi immigrants to London and the growth in independence and modification of culture of a young Bangladeshi woman.<br /><br />The heroine of the book is Nazneen who at the age of 20 enters an arranged marriage with Chanu, age 40, a Banladeshi struggling to establish himself in London. Chanu is striving for a promotion, is proud of his attempts to secure education, and is portrayed at the outset of the book as rather vain and foolish. Bangladeshi society and traditional Islamic practices are patriarchal by western lights, and much is made of this throughout the book. Nanzeen and Chanu have a son, who dies as an infant, and two daughters Shahana and Bibi. Mid-way in the book, with her growth in independence and awareness of her physical and emotional needs, Nanzeen takes a lover, a young man and would-be Islamic radical named Karim. Nanzeen has a beautiful younger sister, Hasina, who remains in Bangladesh, and writes many letters to her sister in a broken English about the course of her life and its hardships.<br /><br />The book gives a portrait of life of the Bangladeshi immigrant community in London. I had no prior knowledge of this community. It describes how the immigrants lived in cramped living conditions in the poorer sections of town with up to ten people per room trying to support themselves in a culture utterly foreign to most of them. Some of the people work at assimilation while others try to retain their religious and Bangladeshi identity. Drugs and violence come to plague the community and, of course, the open sexual mores of modern London prove irresistible to many. The community is shown as divided in its response to the terrorism that has come to dominate world news in recent years.<br /><br />There are a host of well-drawn secondary characters in this novel, including the loan-shark, Mrs. Islam, Nanzeen's lover, named Karim, a friend of Chanu named Dr. Azad, and Razia, a friend of Nanzeen. These characters give weight and texture to the novel and partly succeed in bringing it to life.<br /><br />The book focuses on Nanzeen's development, and the parallel development of Hasina, as it involves Nanzeen's husband, her lover, and, ultimately her independence. It also centers upon Chanu's and Nanzeen's differing desires in terms of returning to Bangladesh.<br /><br />I was intrigued by the excellence of the 100-some discussions by my fellow Amazon reviewers and by the wide divergence of considered responses to this book. Reading the reviews helped me focus upon my own response to the book. Many reviewers found this novel an outstanding first attempt to describe the immigrant experience of the Bangladeshis and the personal growth of the heroine. Other readers found the novel vastly overpraised, difficult, and trite. There is something to be said for both views, but on the whole I agree with the latter opinion. I was happy to have it reinforced by a substantial group of fellow reviewers.<br /><br />The book does portray eloquently the difficulties of the Bangladeshi immigrant community and provokes reflection on how many individuals, in immigrating to a new land, respond to the various choices of assimilation on the one hand and remaining deeply attached to one's initial identity on the other hand. Some of the characters in this book are convincingly drawn. But these are largely the secondary characters, such as Mrs. Islam and Dr. Azad, and also, surprisingly enough, Chanu, Nanzeen's husband.<br /><br />The problems with this book far outweigh its virtues. To begin with, I found it far too long, too slow, and, in many places, dull. It was an unrelieved chore to finish this book. The long sections of letters to Nazneen from her sister Hasina break up the story are difficult to read and detract much more than they add.<br /><br />Equally important, the character on whom Ms. Ali lavishes most of her attention, Nazneen, is trite and unconvincing. I don't think we need another long novel to inform the reader that traditional Islamic society is patriarchal. At times, both at the beginning and at the end of the story, the book does little more than that. Portions of the Nanzeen's development show more depth, as Nanzeen learns of her sexuality and also stands up to Mrs. Islam. But the main theme of the book is predictable and boring and has been done many times in many other contexts.<br /><br />While Nazneen's development shows predictable stereotyping, the author, possibly in spite of herself does a better job with Chanu who for all his faults is a complexly drawn human being deserving of compassion and, perhaps, of a better fate.<br /><br />Thus, in spite of some good moments, I cannot recommend this book due to its length and structure and due to its stereotyped plot line and heroine. I found it worthwhile to review the comments of my fellow reviewers and to offer my own comments here.<br /><br />Robin Friedman</span>
  <a data-text-id="14427790336609882780" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Usually I'm in agreement about a great book, but this one I just don't share the feelings on.<br/>Although i see that other Good Reads readers felt similiarly, which somehow makes me feel better.</span>

<div id="review_64892906" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64892906" />
    <a title="Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1605591-christine"><img alt="Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252166169p2/1605591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64892906?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christine" href="/user/show/1605591-christine">Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=literature-english">literature-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=literature-bangladesh">literature-bangladesh</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=race-issues">race-issues</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=poc-on-cover">poc-on-cover</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=poc-unclear-hero-or-heroine">poc-unclear-hero-or-heroine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=minority-and-women-writers">minority-and-women-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64892906" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4626375916617619002"><i>Brick Lane </i> is an interesting book. The central character, Nazneen is totally passive, almost too passive. It should be noted, however, that Monica Ali does a good job of setting up that passivity. From the very first page of the book, the reader is shown and told that Nazneen is passive, that she was raised to leave things to fate.<br/><br/>The problem with the passiveness of the central character is that it can make the book insufferable, you want her to do. It is here that I have to give Ali points. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText4626375916617619002" style="display:none"><i>Brick Lane </i> is an interesting book. The central character, Nazneen is totally passive, almost too passive. It should be noted, however, that Monica Ali does a good job of setting up that passivity. From the very first page of the book, the reader is shown and told that Nazneen is passive, that she was raised to leave things to fate.<br /><br />The problem with the passiveness of the central character is that it can make the book insufferable, you want her to do. It is here that I have to give Ali points. The bulk of the novel is told though the eyes of Nazneen and when it is not Nazneen we are hearing, it is her sister. Because of these characters and how they were raised, the reader must puzzle things out because the woman are silent on such issues. For instance, what exactly happened to Hasina when she left her first husband. Hasina never directly tells us, but an attentive reader knows. The same is true about Nazneen. It seems that though a good portion of the book Nazneen is heavily depressed. In fact, Ali does a very good job of conveying this, from the state of the house to the state of the food to relationships. This book thrives on the main characters not saying things, and having small details speak. Its interesting, and something I did not find annoying. In many ways, the book is about what takes place in silence.<br /><br /><br />Ali could also make Nazneen's husband Chanu into a brute of a man, but she doesn't. In fact, Chanu is a sympathetic and believable character. When Nazneen hurts him, we feel his pain even though we can understand why Nazneen chose to do what she did. <br /><br /><br />I'm not sure how accurately the book captures the London immigrant's experience, but it does display a good sense of time and space.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4626375916617619002" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review64892906">

<div id="review_1597307263" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1597307263" />
    <a title="G. Lawrence" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38600411-g-lawrence"><img alt="G. Lawrence" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583736125p2/38600411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1597307263?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="G. Lawrence" class="user" itemprop="url" name="G. Lawrence" href="/user/show/38600411-g-lawrence">G. Lawrence</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1597307263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10950737737044845581">Loved this book. The author makes the everyday and often sorrowful events of this woman's life poetic. It is about ordinary life, struggles, hardships, conflicts of faith, duty and culture, and yet for all that it is also beautiful... Giving the details of the good in life which comes with the bad... Showing Nazneen's love for her children, her understanding of god and faith, her sister's search for the life she wants... and although it wasn't always easy to read, it was captivating. The world o</span>
  <span id="freeText10950737737044845581" style="display:none">Loved this book. The author makes the everyday and often sorrowful events of this woman's life poetic. It is about ordinary life, struggles, hardships, conflicts of faith, duty and culture, and yet for all that it is also beautiful... Giving the details of the good in life which comes with the bad... Showing Nazneen's love for her children, her understanding of god and faith, her sister's search for the life she wants... and although it wasn't always easy to read, it was captivating. The world of Nazneen came alive with scents... The lime, the spices, the earth and the rain. And I found myself oddly sorry for Chanu, Nazneen's husband who strives and strives to be known as an educated man, and yet never manages to achieve the dreams he has. Surrounded by his certificates, and never really seeing that his wife is so much more than the "simple village girl" he married, Chanu is as easy to sympathize with , for me, as he was to dislike. His arrogance and inability to see anything beyond his own wishes are so easy to dislike, and yet he fails so many times to become the man he dreams of being that I cannot help but feel for him. To create characters as complex as Chanu and Nazneen, not to mention the host of others, (Mrs Islam being rather like a Dickensian baddie to me) is truly a sign of great talent.<br /><br /> Its the kind of book which demands you pay attention, miss one sentence and you miss a huge part of the story. Its a keeper, going on the book shelf to be read again and again. Really enjoyed this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10950737737044845581" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_973056609" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/973056609" />
    <a title="Yasmin Nessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22170127-yasmin-nessa"><img alt="Yasmin Nessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581291928p2/22170127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/973056609?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yasmin Nessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yasmin Nessa" href="/user/show/22170127-yasmin-nessa">Yasmin Nessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer973056609&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer973056609" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11401989021525417823">One of the most awful books I have ever read. Ignoring the outwardly prejudiced attitude towards Sylhetis by a Dhaka-born writer, Ali chose to further insult the protagonist's culture by allowing the Sylheti community (within the book) to ostensibly reveal these negative stereotypes creating a sense of collective self-hatred. Another plot hole I found was the "broken English" within her sister's letters - the protagonist moved to England and can only say two words in English but her sister, who </span>
  <span id="freeText11401989021525417823" style="display:none">One of the most awful books I have ever read. Ignoring the outwardly prejudiced attitude towards Sylhetis by a Dhaka-born writer, Ali chose to further insult the protagonist's culture by allowing the Sylheti community (within the book) to ostensibly reveal these negative stereotypes creating a sense of collective self-hatred. Another plot hole I found was the "broken English" within her sister's letters - the protagonist moved to England and can only say two words in English but her sister, who did not once leave Bangladesh and experienced an array of upheaval, can write well in English asides from the improper sentence structures? It didn't make sense to me. If it was the letter translated from Bengali, there was really no need to make the translation sound "broken". It made reading the letters seem disingenuous and irritating to follow. The protagonist herself was entirely too passive to be able to pass off as a metaphor and lastly, her writing style was completely dull. The death of her own child was shown completely blank without emotion (unintentionally). Not a single positive thing to say about this book, do not recommend this to anyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11401989021525417823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_19182254" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19182254" />
    <a title="Dale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/329383-dale"><img alt="Dale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1448977473p2/329383.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19182254?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dale" href="/user/show/329383-dale">Dale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19182254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8387531883302553905">This book impressed me because of its immersiveness. Not only in terms of time and place, although that was very well handled, but mostly in terms of character. There are few modern human experiences that could be farther from my own than those of a woman born and raised in Bangladesh relocating to London after an arranged marriage to a man already living there. But I found the main character of Brick Lane, Nazneen, to be very relatable, to the point where I ended up totally immersed in her stor</span>
  <span id="freeText8387531883302553905" style="display:none">This book impressed me because of its immersiveness. Not only in terms of time and place, although that was very well handled, but mostly in terms of character. There are few modern human experiences that could be farther from my own than those of a woman born and raised in Bangladesh relocating to London after an arranged marriage to a man already living there. But I found the main character of Brick Lane, Nazneen, to be very relatable, to the point where I ended up totally immersed in her story and her perspective. That was a pretty heart-wrenching experience, honestly, because Nazneen's story is one of disappointment and fear and powerlessness, right up until just before the very end. This is one of those books which is beautiful, but in which the beauty comes almost totally from sadness. It doesn't exactly have a happy ending in the fairytale sense, but I was pretty pleased with the way things were left by the final page.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8387531883302553905" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1122235913" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1122235913" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3022167-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375273189p2/3022167.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1122235913?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/3022167-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>
